This is defined as the ratio between the\n       <span class="chemf nowrap">\n        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n         14\n         <br/>\n        </span>\n        C\n       </span>\n       /\n       <span class="chemf nowrap">\n        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n         12\n         <br/>\n        </span>\n        C\n       </span>\n       ratio in the sample and the\n       <span class="chemf nowrap">\n        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n         14\n         <br/>\n        </span>\n        C\n       </span>\n       /\n       <span class="chemf nowrap">\n        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n         12\n         <br/>\n        </span>\n        C\n       </span>\n       ratio in modern carbon, which is in turn defined as the\n       <span class="chemf nowrap">\n        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n         14\n         <br/>\n        </span>\n        C\n       </span>\n       /\n       <span class="chemf nowrap">\n        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n         12\n         <br/>\n        </span>\n        C\n       </span>\n       ratio that would have been measured in 1950 had there been no fossil fuel effect.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:11_66-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-:11-66">\n         [60]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Both beta counting and AMS results have to be corrected for fractionation. This is necessary because different materials of the same age, which because of fractionation have naturally different\n       <span class="chemf nowrap">\n        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n         14\n         <br/>\n        </span>\n        C\n       </span>\n       /\n       <span class="chemf nowrap">\n        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n         12\n         <br/>\n        </span>\n        C\n       </span>\n       ratios, will appear to be of different ages because the\n       <span class="chemf nowrap">\n        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n         14\n         <br/>\n        </span>\n        C\n       </span>\n       /\n       <span class="chemf nowrap">\n        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n         12\n         <br/>\n        </span>\n        C\n       </span>\n       ratio is taken as the indicator of age. To avoid this, all radiocarbon measurements are converted to the measurement that would have been seen had the sample been made of wood, which has a known \xce\xb4\n       <span class="chemf nowrap">\n        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n         13\n         <br/>\n        </span>\n        C\n       </span>\n       value of \xe2\x88\x9225\xe2\x80\xb0.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_18-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-:12-18">\n         [17]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Once the corrected\n       <span class="chemf nowrap">\n        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n         14\n         <br/>\n        </span>\n        C\n       </span>\n       /\n       <span class="chemf nowrap">\n        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n         12\n         <br/>\n        </span>\n        C\n       </span>\n       ratio is known, a "radiocarbon age" is calculated using:\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:13_69-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-:13-69">\n         [63]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       <span class="mwe-math-element">\n        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">\n         <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n          <semantics>\n           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n             <mi>\n              A\n             </mi>\n             <mi>\n              g\n             </mi>\n             <mi>\n              e\n             </mi>\n             <mo>\n              =\n             </mo>\n             <mo>\n              \xe2\x88\x92\n              <!-- \xe2\x88\x92 -->\n             </mo>\n             <mn>\n              8033\n             </mn>\n             <mo>\n              \xe2\x8b\x85\n              <!-- \xe2\x8b\x85 -->\n             </mo>\n             <mi>\n              ln\n             </mi>\n             <mo>\n              \xe2\x81\xa1\n              <!-- \xe2\x81\xa1 -->\n             </mo>\n             <mo stretchy="false">\n              (\n             </mo>\n             <mi>\n              F\n             </mi>\n             <mi>\n              m\n             </mi>\n             <mo stretchy="false">\n              )\n             </mo>\n            </mstyle>\n           </mrow>\n           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\n            {\\displaystyle Age=-8033\\cdot \\ln(Fm)}\n           </annotation>\n          </semantics>\n         </math>\n        </span>\n        <img alt="{\\displaystyle Age=-8033\\cdot \\ln(Fm)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/eeb4c842e9317d100073e1449612ede265b1224f" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:22.875ex; height:2.843ex;"/>\n       </span>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The calculation uses Libby\'s half-life of 5,568 years, not the more accurate modern value of 5,730 years. Libby\xe2\x80\x99s value for the half-life is used to maintain consistency with early radiocarbon testing results; calibration curves include a correction for this, so the accuracy of final reported calendar ages is assured.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:13_69-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-:13-69">\n         [63]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Errors_and_reliability">\n        Errors and reliability\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Radiocarbon_dating&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Errors and reliability">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       The reliability of the results can be improved by lengthening the testing time. For example, if counting beta decays for 250 minutes is enough to give an error of \xc2\xb1 80 years, with 68% confidence, then doubling the counting time to 500 minutes will allow a sample with only half as much\n       <span class="chemf nowrap">\n        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n         14\n         <br/>\n        </span>\n        C\n       </span>\n       to be measured with the same error term of 80 years.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowman_38-39_70-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Bowman_38-39-70">\n         [64]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Radiocarbon dating is generally limited to dating samples no more than 50,000 years old, as samples older than that have insufficient\n       <span class="chemf nowrap">\n        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n         14\n         <br/>\n        </span>\n        C\n       </span>\n       to be measurable. Older dates have been obtained by using special sample preparation techniques, large samples, and very long measurement times. These techniques can allow measurement of dates up to 60,000 and in some cases up to 75,000 years before the present.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:14_58-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-:14-58">\n         [52]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Radiocarbon dates are generally presented with a range of one\n       <a href="/wiki/Standard_deviation" title="Standard deviation">\n        standard deviation\n       </a>\n       (usually represented by the Greek letter sigma as 1\xcf\x83) on either side of the mean. However, a date range of 1\xcf\x83 represents only 68% confidence level, so the true age of the object being measured may lie outside the range of dates quoted. This was demonstrated in 1970 by an experiment run by the British Museum radiocarbon laboratory, in which weekly measurements were taken on the same sample for six months. The results varied widely (though consistently with a\n       <a href="/wiki/Normal_distribution" title="Normal distribution">\n        normal distribution\n       </a>\n       of errors in the measurements), and included multiple date ranges (of 1\xcf\x83 confidence) that did not overlap with each other. The measurements included one with a range from about 4250 to about 4390 years ago, and another with a range from about 4520 to about 4690.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">\n        <a href="#cite_note-71">\n         [65]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Errors in procedure can also lead to errors in the results. If 1% of the benzene in a modern reference sample accidentally evaporates, scintillation counting will give a radiocarbon age that is too young by about 80 years.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">\n        <a href="#cite_note-72">\n         [66]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Calibration">\n        Calibration\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Radiocarbon_dating&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Calibration">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Calibration_of_radiocarbon_dates" title="Calibration of radiocarbon dates">\n        Calibration of radiocarbon dates\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tleft">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prometheus_tree1.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="533" data-file-width="796" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Prometheus_tree1.jpg/220px-Prometheus_tree1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Prometheus_tree1.jpg/330px-Prometheus_tree1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Prometheus_tree1.jpg/440px-Prometheus_tree1.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prometheus_tree1.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         The stump of a very old bristlecone pine. Tree rings from these trees (among others) are used in building calibration curves.\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       The calculations given above produce dates in radiocarbon years: i.e. dates that represent the age the sample would be if the\n       <span class="chemf nowrap">\n        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n         14\n         <br/>\n        </span>\n        C\n       </span>\n       /\n       <span class="chemf nowrap">\n        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n         12\n         <br/>\n        </span>\n        C\n       </span>\n       ratio had been constant historically.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">\n        <a href="#cite_note-73">\n         [67]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Although Libby had pointed out as early as 1955 the possibility that this assumption was incorrect, it was not until discrepancies began to accumulate between measured ages and known historical dates for artefacts that it became clear that a correction would need to be applied to radiocarbon ages to obtain calendar dates.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aitken_66-67_74-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Aitken_66-67-74">\n         [68]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       To produce a curve that can be used to relate calendar years to radiocarbon years, a sequence of securely dated samples is needed which can be tested to determine their radiocarbon age. The study of tree rings led to the first such sequence: individual pieces of wood show characteristic sequences of rings that vary in thickness because of environmental factors such as the amount of rainfall in a given year. These factors affect all trees in an area, so examining tree-ring sequences from old wood allows the identification of overlapping sequences. In this way, an uninterrupted sequence of tree rings can be extended far into the past. The first such published sequence, based on bristlecone pine tree rings, was created by\n       <a href="/wiki/Wesley_Ferguson" title="Wesley Ferguson">\n        Wesley Ferguson\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor_.26_Bar-Yosef_.282014.29_32-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Taylor_.26_Bar-Yosef_.282014.29-32">\n         [28]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Hans Suess used this data to publish the first calibration curve for radiocarbon dating in 1967.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowman_16-20_30-5">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Bowman_16-20-30">\n         [26]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Suess_1970_31-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Suess_1970-31">\n         [27]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aitken_66-67_74-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Aitken_66-67-74">\n         [68]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The curve showed two types of variation from the straight line: a long term fluctuation with a period of about 9,000 years, and a shorter term variation, often referred to as "wiggles", with a period of decades. Suess said he drew the line showing the wiggles by "cosmic\n       <i>\n        schwung\n       </i>\n       ", by which he meant that the variations were caused by extraterrestrial forces. It was unclear for some time whether the wiggles were real or not, but they are now well-established.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowman_16-20_30-6">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Bowman_16-20-30">\n         [26]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Suess_1970_31-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Suess_1970-31">\n         [27]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">\n        <a href="#cite_note-75">\n         [69]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       These short term fluctuations in the calibration curve are now known as de Vries effects, after\n       <a href="/wiki/Hessel_de_Vries" title="Hessel de Vries">\n        Hessel de Vries\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">\n        <a href="#cite_note-76">\n         [70]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       A calibration curve is used by taking the radiocarbon date reported by a laboratory, and reading across from that date on the vertical axis of the graph. The point where this horizontal line intersects the curve will give the calendar age of the sample on the horizontal axis. This is the reverse of the way the curve is constructed: a point on the graph is derived from a sample of known age, such as a tree ring; when it is tested, the resulting radiocarbon age gives a data point for the graph.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:18_34-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-:18-34">\n         [30]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Intcal_13_calibration_curve.png">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="710" data-file-width="788" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Intcal_13_calibration_curve.png/220px-Intcal_13_calibration_curve.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Intcal_13_calibration_curve.png/330px-Intcal_13_calibration_curve.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Intcal_13_calibration_curve.png/440px-Intcal_13_calibration_curve.png 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Intcal_13_calibration_curve.png" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         The Northern hemisphere curve from INTCAL13. As of 2014 this is the most recent version of the standard calibration curve. The diagonal line shows where the curve would lie if radiocarbon ages and calendar ages were the same.\n         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-INTCAL13_33-1">\n          <a href="#cite_note-INTCAL13-33">\n           [29]\n          </a>\n         </sup>\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Over the next thirty years many calibration curves were published using a variety of methods and statistical approaches.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:18_34-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-:18-34">\n         [30]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       These were superseded by the INTCAL series of curves, beginning with INTCAL98, published in 1998, and updated in 2004, 2009, and 2013. The improvements to these curves are based on new data gathered from tree rings,\n       <a href="/wiki/Varve" title="Varve">\n        varves\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Coral" title="Coral">\n        coral\n       </a>\n       , plant\n       <a href="/wiki/Macrofossil" title="Macrofossil">\n        macrofossils\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Speleothems" title="Speleothems">\n        speleothems\n       </a>\n       , and\n       <a href="/wiki/Foraminifera" title="Foraminifera">\n        foraminifera\n       </a>\n       . The INTCAL13 data includes separate curves for the northern and southern hemispheres, as they differ systematically because of the hemisphere effect; there is also a separate marine calibration curve.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">\n        <a href="#cite_note-77">\n         [71]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       For a set of samples with a known sequence and separation in time such as a sequence of tree rings, the samples\' radiocarbon ages form a small subset of the calibration curve. The resulting curve can then be matched to the actual calibration curve by identifying where, in the range suggested by the radiocarbon dates, the wiggles in the calibration curve best match the wiggles in the curve of sample dates. This "wiggle-matching" technique can lead to more precise dating than is possible with individual radiocarbon dates.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walker2005_78-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Walker2005-78">\n         [72]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Wiggle-matching can be used in places where there is a plateau on the calibration curve, and hence can provide a much more accurate date than the intercept or probability methods are able to produce.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">\n        <a href="#cite_note-79">\n         [73]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The technique is not restricted to tree rings; for example, a stratified\n       <a href="/wiki/Tephra" title="Tephra">\n        tephra\n       </a>\n       sequence in New Zealand, known to predate human colonization of the islands, has been dated to 1314\xc2\xa0AD \xc2\xb1 12 years by wiggle-matching.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">\n        <a href="#cite_note-80">\n         [74]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The wiggles also mean that reading a date from a calibration curve can give more than one answer: this occurs when the curve wiggles up and down enough that the radiocarbon age intercepts the curve in more than one place, which may lead to a radiocarbon result being reported as two separate age ranges, corresponding to the two parts of the curve that the radiocarbon age intercepted.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:18_34-3">\n        <a href="#cite_note-:18-34">\n         [30]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       <a href="/wiki/Bayesian_inference" title="Bayesian inference">\n        Bayesian statistical techniques\n       </a>\n       can be applied when there are several radiocarbon dates to be calibrated. For example, if a series of radiocarbon dates is taken from different levels in a given stratigraphic sequence, Bayesian analysis can help determine if some of the dates should be discarded as anomalies, and can use the information to improve the output probability distributions.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walker2005_78-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Walker2005-78">\n         [72]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       When Bayesian analysis was introduced, its use was limited by the need to use mainframe computers to perform the calculations, but the technique has since been implemented on programs available for personal computers, such as OxCal.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">\n        <a href="#cite_note-81">\n         [75]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Reporting_dates">\n        Reporting dates\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Radiocarbon_dating&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Reporting dates">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       Several formats for citing radiocarbon results have been used since the first samples were dated. As of 2014, the standard format required by the journal\n       <i>\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_(magazine)" title="Radiocarbon (magazine)">\n         Radiocarbon\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       is as follows.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Radiocarbon_Authors_82-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Radiocarbon_Authors-82">\n         [76]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Uncalibrated dates should be reported as "&lt;laboratory&gt;: &lt;\n       <span class="chemf nowrap">\n        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n         14\n         <br/>\n        </span>\n        C\n       </span>\n       year&gt; \xc2\xb1 &lt;range&gt; BP", where:\n      </p>\n      <ul>\n       <li>\n        &lt;laboratory&gt; identifies the laboratory that tested the sample, and the sample ID\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        &lt;\n        <span class="chemf nowrap">\n         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n          14\n          <br/>\n         </span>\n         C\n        </span>\n        year&gt; is the laboratory\'s determination of the age of the sample, in radiocarbon years\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        &lt;range&gt; is the laboratory\'s estimate of the error in the age, at 1\xcf\x83 confidence.\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        BP stands for "\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Before_present" title="Before present">\n         before present\n        </a>\n        ", referring to a reference date of 1950, so that 500 BP means the year 1450 AD.\n       </li>\n      </ul>\n      <p>\n       For example, the uncalibrated date "UtC-2020: 3510 \xc2\xb1 60 BP" indicates that the sample was tested by the Utrecht van der Graaf Laboratorium, where it has a sample number of 2020, and that the uncalibrated age is 3510 years before present, \xc2\xb1 60 years. Related forms are sometimes used: for example, "10 ka BP" means 10,000 radiocarbon years before present (i.e. 8,050 BC), and\n       <span class="chemf nowrap">\n        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n         14\n         <br/>\n        </span>\n        C\n       </span>\n       yr BP might be used to distinguish the uncalibrated date from a date derived from another dating method such as\n       <a href="/wiki/Thermoluminescence_dating" title="Thermoluminescence dating">\n        thermoluminescence\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Radiocarbon_Authors_82-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Radiocarbon_Authors-82">\n         [76]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Calibrated\n       <span class="chemf nowrap">\n        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n         14\n         <br/>\n        </span>\n        C\n       </span>\n       dates are frequently reported as cal BP, cal BC, or cal AD, again with BP referring to the year 1950 as the zero date.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">\n        <a href="#cite_note-83">\n         [77]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <i>\n        Radiocarbon\n       </i>\n       gives two options for reporting calibrated dates. A common format is "cal &lt;date-range&gt; &lt;confidence&gt;", where:\n      </p>\n      <ul>\n       <li>\n        &lt;date-range&gt; is the range of dates corresponding to the given confidence level\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        &lt;confidence&gt; indicates the confidence level for the given date range.\n       </li>\n      </ul>\n      <p>\n       For example, "cal 1220\xe2\x80\x931281 AD (1\xcf\x83)" means a calibrated date for which the true date lies between 1220 AD and 1281 AD, with the confidence level given as 1\xcf\x83, or one standard deviation. Calibrated dates can also be expressed as BP instead of using BC and AD. The curve used to calibrate the results should be the latest available INTCAL curve. Calibrated dates should also identify any programs, such as OxCal, used to perform the calibration.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Radiocarbon_Authors_82-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Radiocarbon_Authors-82">\n         [76]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In addition, an article in\n       <i>\n        Radiocarbon\n       </i>\n       in 2014 about radiocarbon date reporting conventions recommends that information should be provided about sample treatment, including the sample material, pretreatment methods, and quality control measurements; that the citation to the software used for calibration should specify the version number and any options or models used; and that the calibrated date should be given with the associated probabilities for each range.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">\n        <a href="#cite_note-84">\n         [78]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Use_in_archaeology">\n        Use in archaeology\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Radiocarbon_dating&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Use in archaeology">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Interpretation">\n        Interpretation\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Radiocarbon_dating&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Interpretation">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       A key concept in interpreting radiocarbon dates is\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaeological_association" title="Archaeological association">\n        archaeological association\n       </a>\n       : what is the true relationship between two or more objects at an archaeological site? It frequently happens that a sample for radiocarbon dating can be taken directly from the object of interest, but there are also many cases where this is not possible. Metal grave goods, for example, cannot be radiocarbon dated, but they may be found in a grave with a coffin, charcoal, or other material which can be assumed to have been deposited at the same time. In these cases a date for the coffin or charcoal is indicative of the date of deposition of the grave goods, because of the direct functional relationship between the two. There are also cases where there is no functional relationship, but the association is reasonably strong: for example, a layer of charcoal in a rubbish pit provides a date which has a relationship to the rubbish pit.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">\n        <a href="#cite_note-85">\n         [79]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Contamination is of particular concern when dating very old material obtained from archaeological excavations and great care is needed in the specimen selection and preparation. In 2014, Tom Higham and co-workers suggested that many of the dates published for\n       <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">\n        Neanderthal\n       </a>\n       artefacts are too recent because of contamination by "young carbon".\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">\n        <a href="#cite_note-86">\n         [80]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       As a tree grows, only the outermost tree ring exchanges carbon with its environment, so the age measured for a wood sample depends on where the sample is taken from. This means that radiocarbon dates on wood samples can be older than the date at which the tree was felled. In addition, if a piece of wood is used for multiple purposes, there may be a significant delay between the felling of the tree and the final use in the context in which it is found.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_87-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-:17-87">\n         [81]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       This is often referred to as the "\n       <a href="/wiki/Old_wood" title="Old wood">\n        old wood\n       </a>\n       " problem.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowman_9-15_5-16">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Bowman_9-15-5">\n         [5]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       One example is the Bronze Age\n       <a href="/wiki/Trackway" title="Trackway">\n        trackway\n       </a>\n       at Withy Bed Copse, in England; the trackway was built from wood that had clearly been worked for other purposes before being re-used in the trackway. Another example is driftwood, which may be used as construction material. It is not always possible to recognize re-use. Other materials can present the same problem: for example,\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bitumen" title="Bitumen">\n        bitumen\n       </a>\n       is known to have been used by some\n       <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">\n        Neolithic\n       </a>\n       communities to waterproof baskets; the bitumen\'s radiocarbon age will be greater than is measurable by the laboratory, regardless of the actual age of the context, so testing the basket material will give a misleading age if care is not taken. A separate issue, related to re-use, is that of lengthy use, or delayed deposition. For example, a wooden object that remains in use for a lengthy period will have an apparent age greater than the actual age of the context in which it is deposited.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_87-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-:17-87">\n         [81]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_applications">\n        Notable applications\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Radiocarbon_dating&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Notable applications">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <h4>\n       <span id="Pleistocene/Holocene_boundary_in_Two_Creeks_Fossil_Forest">\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Pleistocene.2FHolocene_boundary_in_Two_Creeks_Fossil_Forest">\n        Pleistocene/Holocene boundary in Two Creeks Fossil Forest\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Radiocarbon_dating&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Pleistocene/Holocene boundary in Two Creeks Fossil Forest">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h4>\n      <p>\n       The\n       <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">\n        Pleistocene\n       </a>\n       is a geological epoch that began about 2.6 million years ago. The\n       <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">\n        Holocene\n       </a>\n       , the current geological epoch, begins about 11,700 years ago, when the Pleistocene ends.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:15_88-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-:15-88">\n         [82]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Establishing the date of this boundary \xe2\x88\x92 which is defined by sharp climatic warming \xe2\x88\x92 as accurately as possible has been a goal of geologists for much of the 20th century.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:15_88-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-:15-88">\n         [82]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">\n        <a href="#cite_note-89">\n         [83]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       At\n       <a href="/wiki/Two_Creeks,_Wisconsin" title="Two Creeks, Wisconsin">\n        Two Creeks\n       </a>\n       , in Wisconsin, a fossil forest was discovered (\n       <a href="/wiki/Two_Creeks_Buried_Forest_State_Natural_Area" title="Two Creeks Buried Forest State Natural Area">\n        Two Creeks Buried Forest State Natural Area\n       </a>\n       ), and subsequent research determined that the destruction of the forest was caused by the Valders ice readvance, the last southward movement of ice before the end of the Pleistocene in that area. Before the advent of radiocarbon dating, the fossilized trees had been dated by correlating sequences of annually deposited layers of sediment at Two Creeks with sequences in Scandinavia. This led to estimates that the trees were between 24,000 and 19,000 years old,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:15_88-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-:15-88">\n         [82]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and hence this was taken to be the date of the last advance of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin_glaciation" title="Wisconsin glaciation">\n        Wisconsin glaciation\n       </a>\n       before its final retreat marked the end of the Pleistocene in North America.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:16_90-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-:16-90">\n         [84]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In 1952 Libby published radiocarbon dates for several samples from the Two Creeks site and two similar sites nearby; the dates were averaged to 11,404 BP with a standard error of 350 years. This result was uncalibrated, as the need for calibration of radiocarbon ages was not yet understood. Further results over the next decade supported an average date of 11,350 BP, with the results thought to be most accurate averaging 11,600 BP. There was initial resistance to these results on the part of\n       <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Antevs" title="Ernst Antevs">\n        Ernst Antevs\n       </a>\n       , the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palaeobotanist" title="Palaeobotanist">\n        palaeobotanist\n       </a>\n       who had worked on the Scandinavian varve series, but his objections were eventually discounted by other geologists. In the 1990s samples were tested with AMS, yielding (uncalibrated) dates ranging from 11,640 BP to 11,800 BP, both with a standard error of 160 years. Subsequently, a sample from the fossil forest was used in an interlaboratory test, with results provided by over 70 laboratories. These tests produced a median age of 11,788 \xc2\xb1 8 BP (2\xcf\x83 confidence) which when calibrated gives a date range of 13,730 to 13,550 cal BP.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:15_88-3">\n        <a href="#cite_note-:15-88">\n         [82]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The Two Creeks radiocarbon dates are now regarded as a key result in developing the modern understanding of North American glaciation at the end of the Pleistocene.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:16_90-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-:16-90">\n         [84]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h4>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Dead_Sea_Scrolls">\n        Dead Sea Scrolls\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Radiocarbon_dating&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Dead Sea Scrolls">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h4>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Great_Isaiah_Scroll_Ch53.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2319" data-file-width="2040" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Great_Isaiah_Scroll_Ch53.jpg/220px-Great_Isaiah_Scroll_Ch53.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Great_Isaiah_Scroll_Ch53.jpg/330px-Great_Isaiah_Scroll_Ch53.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Great_Isaiah_Scroll_Ch53.jpg/440px-Great_Isaiah_Scroll_Ch53.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Great_Isaiah_Scroll_Ch53.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Part of the Great Isaiah Scroll, one of the Dead Sea Scrolls\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       In 1947,\n       <a href="/wiki/Dead_Sea_Scrolls" title="Dead Sea Scrolls">\n        scrolls\n       </a>\n       were discovered in caves near the\n       <a href="/wiki/Dead_Sea" title="Dead Sea">\n        Dead Sea\n       </a>\n       that proved to contain writing in\n       <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">\n        Hebrew\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_language" title="Aramaic language">\n        Aramaic\n       </a>\n       , most of which are thought to have been produced by the\n       <a href="/wiki/Essenes" title="Essenes">\n        Essenes\n       </a>\n       , a small Jewish sect. These scrolls are of great significance in the study of Biblical texts because many of them contain the earliest known version of books of the Hebrew bible.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor38_91-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-taylor38-91">\n         [85]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       A sample of the linen wrapping from one of these scrolls, the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Isaiah_scroll" title="Great Isaiah scroll">\n        Great Isaiah Scroll\n       </a>\n       , was included in a 1955 analysis by Libby, with an estimated age of 1,917 \xc2\xb1 200 years.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor38_91-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-taylor38-91">\n         [85]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">\n        <a href="#cite_note-92">\n         [86]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Based on an analysis of the writing style,\n       <a href="/wiki/Palaeography" title="Palaeography">\n        palaeographic\n       </a>\n       estimates were made of the age of 21 of the scrolls, and samples from most of these, along with other scrolls which had not been palaeographically dated, were tested by two AMS laboratories in the 1990s. The results ranged in age from the early 4th century BC to the mid 4th century AD. In many cases the scrolls were determined to be older than the palaeographically determined age. The Isaiah scroll was included in the testing and was found to have two possible date ranges at a 2\xcf\x83 confidence level, because of the shape of the calibration curve at that point: there is a 15% chance that it dates from 355\xe2\x80\x93295 BC, and an 84% chance that it dates from 210\xe2\x80\x9345 BC. Subsequently, these dates were criticized on the grounds that before the scrolls were tested, they had been treated with modern castor oil in order to make the writing easier to read; it was argued that failure to remove the castor oil sufficiently would have caused the dates to be too young. Multiple papers have been published both supporting and opposing the criticism.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor38_91-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-taylor38-91">\n         [85]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Impact">\n        Impact\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Radiocarbon_dating&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Impact">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       Soon after the publication of Libby\'s 1949 paper in\n       <i>\n        Science\n       </i>\n       , universities around the world began establishing radiocarbon-dating laboratories, and by the end of the 1950s there were more than 20 active\n       <span class="chemf nowrap">\n        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n         14\n         <br/>\n        </span>\n        C\n       </span>\n       research laboratories. It quickly became apparent that the principles of radiocarbon dating were valid, despite certain discrepancies, the causes of which then remained unknown.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">\n        <a href="#cite_note-93">\n         [87]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The development of radiocarbon dating has had a profound impact on archaeology\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x93 often described as the "radiocarbon revolution".\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">\n        <a href="#cite_note-94">\n         [88]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In the words of anthropologist R.\xc2\xa0E. Taylor, "\n       <span class="chemf nowrap">\n        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n         14\n         <br/>\n        </span>\n        C\n       </span>\n       data made a\n       <i>\n        world\n       </i>\n       prehistory possible by contributing a time scale that transcends local, regional and continental boundaries". It provides more accurate dating within sites than previous methods, which usually derived either from stratigraphy or from typologies (e.g. of stone tools or pottery); it also allows comparison and synchronization of events across great distances. The advent of radiocarbon dating may even have led to better field methods in archaeology, since better data recording leads to firmer association of objects with the samples to be tested. These improved field methods were sometimes motivated by attempts to prove that a\n       <span class="chemf nowrap">\n        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n         14\n         <br/>\n        </span>\n        C\n       </span>\n       date was incorrect. Taylor also suggests that the availability of definite date information freed archaeologists from the need to focus so much of their energy on determining the dates of their finds, and led to an expansion of the questions archaeologists were willing to research. For example, from the 1970s questions about the evolution of human behaviour were much more frequently seen in archaeology.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:19_95-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-:19-95">\n         [89]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The dating framework provided by radiocarbon led to a change in the prevailing view of how innovations spread through prehistoric Europe. Researchers had previously thought that many ideas spread by diffusion through the continent, or by invasions of peoples bringing new cultural ideas with them. As radiocarbon dates began to prove these ideas wrong in many instances, it became apparent that these innovations must sometimes have arisen locally. This has been described as a "second radiocarbon revolution", and with regard to British prehistory, archaeologist\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Richard_J.C._Atkinson" title="Richard J.C. Atkinson">\n        Richard Atkinson\n       </a>\n       has characterized the impact of radiocarbon dating as "radical\xc2\xa0... therapy" for the "progressive disease of invasionism". More broadly, the success of radiocarbon dating stimulated interest in analytical and statistical approaches to archaeological data.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:19_95-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-:19-95">\n         [89]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Taylor has also described the impact of AMS, and the ability to obtain accurate measurements from very small samples, as ushering in a third radiocarbon revolution.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">\n        <a href="#cite_note-96">\n         [90]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Occasionally, radiocarbon dating techniques date an object of popular interest, for example the\n       <a href="/wiki/Shroud_of_Turin" title="Shroud of Turin">\n        Shroud of Turin\n       </a>\n       , a piece of linen cloth thought by some to bear an image of Jesus Christ after his crucifixion. Three separate laboratories\n       <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating_of_the_Shroud_of_Turin" title="Radiocarbon dating of the Shroud of Turin">\n        dated samples of linen from the Shroud in 1988\n       </a>\n       ; the results pointed to 14th-century origins, raising doubts about the shroud\'s authenticity as an alleged 1st-century relic.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Currie_2004_13-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Currie_2004-13">\n         [13]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Researchers have studied other radioactive isotopes created by cosmic rays to determine if they could also be used to assist in dating objects of archaeological interest; such isotopes include\n       <a href="/wiki/Helium-3" title="Helium-3">\n        <span class="chemf nowrap">\n         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n          3\n          <br/>\n         </span>\n         He\n        </span>\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Beryllium-10" title="Beryllium-10">\n        <span class="chemf nowrap">\n         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n          10\n          <br/>\n         </span>\n         Be\n        </span>\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neon-21" title="Neon-21">\n        <span class="chemf nowrap">\n         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n          21\n          <br/>\n         </span>\n         Ne\n        </span>\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Aluminium-26" title="Aluminium-26">\n        <span class="chemf nowrap">\n         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n          26\n          <br/>\n         </span>\n         Al\n        </span>\n       </a>\n       , and\n       <a href="/wiki/Chlorine-36" title="Chlorine-36">\n        <span class="chemf nowrap">\n         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n          36\n          <br/>\n         </span>\n         Cl\n        </span>\n       </a>\n       . With the development of AMS in the 1980s it became possible to measure these isotopes precisely enough for them to be the basis of useful dating techniques, which have been primarily applied to dating rocks.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">\n        <a href="#cite_note-97">\n         [91]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Naturally occurring radioactive isotopes can also form the basis of dating methods, as with\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/K-Ar_dating" title="K-Ar dating">\n        potassium\xe2\x80\x93argon dating\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Argon%E2%80%93argon_dating" title="Argon\xe2\x80\x93argon dating">\n        argon\xe2\x80\x93argon dating\n       </a>\n       , and\n       <a href="/wiki/Uranium%E2%80%93thorium_dating" title="Uranium\xe2\x80\x93thorium dating">\n        uranium series dating\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">\n        <a href="#cite_note-98">\n         [92]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Other dating techniques of interest to archaeologists include\n       <a href="/wiki/Thermoluminescence_dating" title="Thermoluminescence dating">\n        thermoluminescence\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Optically_stimulated_luminescence" title="Optically stimulated luminescence">\n        optically stimulated luminescence\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_spin_resonance" title="Electron spin resonance">\n        electron spin resonance\n       </a>\n       , and\n       <a href="/wiki/Fission_track_dating" title="Fission track dating">\n        fission track dating\n       </a>\n       , as well as techniques that depend on annual bands or layers, such as\n       <a href="/wiki/Dendrochronology" title="Dendrochronology">\n        dendrochronology\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Tephrochronology" title="Tephrochronology">\n        tephrochronology\n       </a>\n       , and\n       <a href="/wiki/Varve" title="Varve">\n        varve\n       </a>\n       chronology.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">\n        <a href="#cite_note-99">\n         [93]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In 2016, the development of radiocarbon dating was recognized as a\n       <a href="/wiki/National_Historic_Chemical_Landmarks" title="National Historic Chemical Landmarks">\n        National Historic Chemical Landmark\n       </a>\n       for its contributions to chemistry and society by the\n       <a href="/wiki/American_Chemical_Society" title="American Chemical Society">\n        American Chemical Society\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">\n        <a href="#cite_note-100">\n         [94]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">\n        See also\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Radiocarbon_dating&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: See also">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <ul>\n       <li>\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dating_methodologies_in_archaeology" title="Dating methodologies in archaeology">\n         Dating methodologies in archaeology\n        </a>\n       </li>\n      </ul>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">\n        Notes\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Radiocarbon_dating&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Notes">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n       <ol class="references">\n        <li id="cite_note-16">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          <b>\n           <a href="#cite_ref-16">\n            ^\n           </a>\n          </b>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          The mean-life and half-life are related by the following equation:\n          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowman_9-15_5-8">\n           <a href="#cite_note-Bowman_9-15-5">\n            [5]\n           </a>\n          </sup>\n         </span>\n         <dl>\n          <dd>\n           <span class="reference-text">\n            <span class="mwe-math-element">\n             <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">\n              <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n               <semantics>\n                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                 <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n                  <msub>\n                   <mi>\n                    T\n                   </mi>\n                   <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                    <mfrac>\n                     <mn>\n                      1\n                     </mn>\n                     <mn>\n                      2\n                     </mn>\n                    </mfrac>\n                   </mrow>\n                  </msub>\n                  <mo>\n                   =\n                  </mo>\n                  <mn>\n                   0.693\n                  </mn>\n                  <mo>\n                   \xe2\x8b\x85\n                   <!-- \xe2\x8b\x85 -->\n                  </mo>\n                  <mi>\n                   \xcf\x84\n                   <!-- \xcf\x84 -->\n                  </mi>\n                 </mstyle>\n                </mrow>\n                <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\n                 {\\displaystyle T_{\\frac {1}{2}}=0.693\\cdot \\tau }\n                </annotation>\n               </semantics>\n              </math>\n             </span>\n             <img alt="T_{\\frac {1}{2}}=0.693\\cdot \\tau " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cdddecc1f91d714429399c29dda14c65539b2cf1" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:14.47ex; height:4.009ex;"/>\n            </span>\n           </span>\n          </dd>\n         </dl>\n        </li>\n        <li id="cite_note-21">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          <b>\n           <a href="#cite_ref-21">\n            ^\n           </a>\n          </b>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          The term "conventional radiocarbon age" is also used. The definition of radiocarbon years is as follows: the age is calculated by using the following\n          <a href="/wiki/Calculation_of_radiocarbon_dates#Standards" title="Calculation of radiocarbon dates">\n           standards\n          </a>\n          : a) using the Libby half-life of 5568 years, rather than the currently accepted actual half-life of 5730 years; (b) the use of an NIST standard known as HOxII to define the activity of radiocarbon in 1950; (c) the use of 1950 as the date from which years "before present" are counted; (d) a correction for\n          <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating#Isotopic_fractionation" title="Radiocarbon dating">\n           fractionation\n          </a>\n          , based on a standard isotope ratio, and (e) the assumption that the\n          <span class="chemf nowrap">\n           <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n            14\n            <br/>\n           </span>\n           C\n          </span>\n          /\n          <span class="chemf nowrap">\n           <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">\n            12\n            <br/>\n           </span>\n           C\n          </span>\n          ratio has not changed over time.\n          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor_4_20-0">\n           <a href="#cite_note-Taylor_4-20">\n            [19]\n           </a>\n          </sup>\n         </span>\n        </li>\n        <li id="cite_note-23">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          <b>\n           <a href="#cite_ref-23">\n            ^\n           </a>\n          </b>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          The data on carbon percentages in each part of the reservoir is drawn from an estimate of reservoir carbon for the mid-1990s; estimates of carbon distribution during pre-industrial times are significantly different.\n          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GC_128-9_22-0">\n           <a href="#cite_note-GC_128-9-22">\n            [20]\n           </a>\n          </sup>\n         </span>\n        </li>\n        <li id="cite_note-29">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          <b>\n           <a href="#cite_ref-29">\n            ^\n           </a>\n          </b>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          The age only appears to be 440 years once a correction for\n          <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating#Fractionation" title="Radiocarbon dating">\n           fractionation\n          </a>\n          is made. This effect is accounted for during calibration by using a different marine calibration curve; without this curve, modern marine life would appear to be 440 years old when radiocarbon dated.\n         </span>\n        </li>\n        <li id="cite_note-42">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          <b>\n           <a href="#cite_ref-42">\n            ^\n           </a>\n          </b>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          "PDB" stands for "Pee Dee Belemnite", a fossil from the\n          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pee_Dee_Formation" title="Pee Dee Formation">\n           Pee Dee formation\n          </a>\n          in South Carolina.\n          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">\n           <a href="#cite_note-41">\n            [37]\n           </a>\n          </sup>\n         </span>\n        </li>\n        <li id="cite_note-44">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          <b>\n           <a href="#cite_ref-44">\n            ^\n           </a>\n          </b>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          The PDB value is 11.2372\xe2\x80\xb0.\n          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">\n           <a href="#cite_note-43">\n            [38]\n           </a>\n          </sup>\n         </span>\n        </li>\n       </ol>\n      </div>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="References">\n        References\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Radiocarbon_dating&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: References">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 20em;           <span style="display:none;">\n           </span>\n          </span>\n         </span>\n        </li>\n        <li id="cite_note-Bowman_9-15-5">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          ^\n          <a href="#cite_ref-Bowman_9-15_5-0">\n           <sup>\n            <i>\n             <b>\n              a\n             </b>\n            </i>\n           </sup>\n          </a>\n          <a href="#cite_ref-Bowman_9-15_5-1">\n           <sup>\n            <i>\n             <b>\n              b\n             </b>\n            </i>\n           </sup>\n          </a>\n          <a href="#cite_ref-Bowman_9-15_5-2">\n           <sup>\n            <i>\n             <b>\n              c\n             </b>\n            </i>\n           </sup>\n          </a>\n          <a
